And puncturing does indeed draw blood from your veins, scattered in the flesh, and for that reason, force does not fail from it, just as is the case with hemorrhage. If necessity calls for puncturing from a disease, or out of custom, we administer it at any time in the beginning of the month, at its end, and in the middle of it, and in whatever time there may be.
That is because when blood multiplies in a person, until an extraction of it by puncturing is needed, it is found in the head of his scalp, and sores.

Chapter 2.
It is necessary that we say in the preface of this chapter, that this is the highest sermon, and the chapter, to whose understanding the artificer first directs his gaze: namely, that you, and those who are quick, may know, or learn this noble art, and others from you.
I say that when a fracture, or dislocation, or twist, or case occurs in anyone, it is necessary that we immediately attend to cutting and opening the veins, and letting out the blood, or both at once: if nothing forbids, such as weakness of the virtues: or is he who has this occur to him a child, or an old man, or is it a time of excessive heat, or cold.
Then he should be content with his food, with cold vegetables, and with flesh of animals, and of birds: and forbid wine, and fat meat, and all food that fills the belly with blood. When the broken bone begins to be restored, then it is necessary that the weak one be nourished with food nutritive, much and coarse and heavy in which there is dough: such as bread made from cooked flour and water, and rice, and peas, and beans, and onions, and fish recent, and wine gross, and such like. When this regimen is made for the coagulation of the fracture to be more quickly and better.
And you know that bones fractured when they are in the young, who are in the beginning of old age, and those who are decrepit, cannot continue and consolidate according to the nature of their own, because of the dryness of their members: and they become solid and remain that which is at the end of their relief, as the bones of young boys.

And you know that the species of fractures are diverse, according to the diversity of the limbs: for the fracture of the thigh is different from the fracture of the bone of the skull: and the fracture of the bone of the breast is different from the fracture of the bone of the back: and similarly, all other limbs are diverse in their nature: and I will come with a reminder of all the species of fractures, distinct from another, in its own chapter. Regarding the accidental event in the head.
The species of fractures are diverse, and the figures of them are many: their causes are distinct.
For it is a fracture that is made by a blow with an hammer: and the blow strikes the bone entirely until it reaches the siphac, which is under the osse, just as an axle does in a tree: and for this reason this species of fracture is called the axial one.
Or the hammer strikes some bones, and it hits their face, and it does not reach the end of them by the incision: and this species of fracture is called the Kalaan, that is, the absolute laceration.
And it is a joint of two fractures, either great or small.
And it is a fracture that is made by a grinding or attrition: and its cause is a blow with a stone, or a chance above the stone, or such things.
And this fracture is also a penetrating one, which is approaching the gut, which is under the osse: or it is in the face of the bones: and it is a joint of two fractures, either great or small.
And it is a fracture that is made by a hidden one in the bone in the subtlety of the hair: and it is a small fissure, and for this reason this species of fracture is called the Capillaris.
And among them is a fracture that is made by a chance, or a blow with a stone, and such things which make the layer of the bone to be able to go into the interior, and it is a place of cohesion: just as happens when the hot air meets with them by a blow, and its part goes into the interior.
Indeed, this is particularly true in the cases of wet bones, such as those of children.
Indeed, all these kinds of dislocations are separated, and I recall this with reference to the whole in its proper place.
Indeed, we recognize all these kinds of dislocations by detection on the part affected, and examination of their parts when touched, and removal of the corrupted flesh that is above them.
Indeed, the species of hair is recognized by this, that it is detected on the bone, and removed, and encased around it with wax: for a black discoloration appears in this case.
In the treatment of the fracture, consider first the accidents of the weak: then if you see accidents that are manifestly dangerous, such as diarrhea, and extension, and loss of intellect, and glottis abscess, and syncope, and acute fever, and exit of eyes, and redness of them, and similar accidents in many dispositions, it is unlikely that it will be cured: and if you see accidents that do not frighten you and hope for health, then begin the care of it.
Indeed, if a wound comes to you first when wounded: and this is in the days of winter, then it is necessary that you study in the removal of bones before the fourth day of every disposition.
Indeed, if it is in the days of summer, then it is necessary that you remove the bone before the seventh day, before the corruption that is under the bone from the bandage, and those accidents that we have mentioned occur.
Indeed, if the fracture of the bones has already healed to touching the brain with minutiae, and is with attraction: then it is necessary that you incur the part which is comminuted and attrited, according to what I tell you.
It is said that the head of a wounded person should be treated like this: and grasp it over the eyes in whatever manner possible to you, and according to the shape of the wound, and what is needed over the weakest part.
If it happens to you during the detection of bone fluid or hot clot: then place it with it as is necessary. And it is said that you should fill the place with poultices made from wet bandages in wine, and rose oil: and make the knee quiet until the clot is settled, and be secure from the flow of blood. Then begin with the treatment of the bone, and removal of it.
And this is done according to one of two modes.
Indeed, one mode is to cut the bone with a sharp edge: and this is its shape.
From there, you will administer another Incisor wider than the first one: and this is its shape.
Then, you will also administer another Incisor wider than the previous one.
And the second mode is to have a number of different Incisors with you: some of which are wider than others, and some of which are narrower than others: and they should be at the very end of their sharpness, and should be made of iron or steel: and use your ease in percussion on the Incisor, so that it does not hurt the head, and harms the itself.
However, if the bone is hard and tough: then it is necessary to make a hole in its circumference, before you administer the Incisors, with Terebrae which are called Terebra, not because they are deep: and they are not called so for this reason, because they do not reach the end of the bone flesh after it: therefore, because the Terebra is the round edge above it, which is under the sharp edge of the head, similar to the margin, and the smaller circle prevents it from being submerged, and passing through the density of the bone.
When you are ready, take a large number of these terebrae from them, as many as there are bones of sufficient thickness to fill the entire cranium of Terebrum, whose sharp extremities in length and breadth should be proportional to the thickness of the bone of Terebrum.
This is the form of three kinds of Terebrums, or trephines.
The quality of drilling is such that you will place the trephine on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been completely pierced. Then rotate the trephine to another place and place it where there is a space between all the holes of about this size.
Then drill with chisels that are between all the holes of the bone: and make it finer than you can with your hand, or with any other instrument you have prepared for this purpose, such as trowels and thin chisels.
And it is necessary that you take all care not to touch the trephine with the chisel or anything else from the plaster.
When you have drilled the bone all around and separated it from the plaster, if it is continuous with it, and taken it out, it is necessary that you make rays and equal ends of that which remains in the bone with another similar chisel. However, it should be more thin and delicate with other chisels.
If something remains of the bones that are similar to those that were cut out with the chisels, then take it with as much ease as there is with the instruments you have. Then take care of the wound after that with ointments and unguents as I have mentioned.
The method of another operation is easy, but long due to fear: which Galen said and praised greatly, and this is his speech. First of all, you must begin to expose a part of the bone from the location where the fracture is most severe and apparent: then, when you have exposed that part and placed it under the extremity of this Incisorii lenticularis tooth, whose shape this is.
And the lenticular part does not cut anything gently: and the sharp part of it is curved on two sides of its handle in length, as you see: so that the lenticular part may be fixed to the siphac, with its sharp side facing the bone.
Then, strike it with your little hammer from the one side with the Incisorium, until you cut the bone with ease, as it rotates: and you will be secure from the bandage, which does not happen in that happening, which you fear greatly.
And if the physician were not a foolish man, and more ignorant of this, nor sleeping, then if anything was left attached to the bone from the bandage in some places, he would cut it off with the sharp end of the Incisorium lenticularis of his own tooth, and free it from it with ease. He is freed from it without harm, and without fear.

¶ About the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
You know that the nose is not broken unless two of its upper parts are broken at once, or one of them: for they are two bones: therefore, because they are two bones, it does not break the lower part which is cartilage, and it does not happen to it except wearing, and twisting, and flatness.
If one of its upper parts is broken, then you must insert a small finger into the hole of the nose, and mark the fracture with your own thumb, and with the bent knuckle, until the nose returns to its natural figure: and it should be made artificially with ease. Because lest it happens through your operation on that weak pain.
If a fracture is in the upper part of the nose and does not extend to the fingers: then it is necessary that it be equalized at the end of the radius in which there is small growth.
If indeed the fracture is in two parts, then make it similar to that: and soon after its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day at quiet, when there is heat, you should stitch it.
Then insert into the end of the nose a linen bandage, if the fracture is in one part: or two linen bandages if the fracture is in two parts: and let the growth in the bandage be as much as to fill the opening of the nose.
And certain ancient restorers said that the bandage should be smeared with butter and turned every day. This does not seem to me, but rather it is necessary that the bandage be smeared with almond oil and complete with flour when it is finished. Then release the bandage until the bone is firm and the cartilage is hardened.
And when it is inserted into the nose at the place of the goose quill, after it has been wrapped around the bandage: and it happens that it holds the fracture of the nose more firmly, and that it does not prevent the weak one from inspiration.
And this is not necessary. If you wish, do this: and if you wish, make the bandage.
If a fracture happens in the nose again while you are stitching, apply to the nose plaster with powder, or with powder infused in rose oil, or a little of dyaquilon.
If, however, a hot sty does not occur, then it is necessary to apply a plaster made with flour and powdered turmeric mixed with gum and alginate. Then place a soft cloth over the plaster and do not push the nose against anything at all.
If, however, the bones of the nose are broken or shattered, then it is necessary to cut over them and take them out with the instruments that come together with them. Then make a cut and take care of it with the part that is forming and being solidified from the plasters suitable for that, or ointments.
If, however, it occurs between the nose and the cheek, then it is necessary to treat it with liniments: and give a needle of lead until it heals.
¶ Regarding the restoration of the lower jaw when it is broken,
When the lower jaw is broken and it is not broken with the cheek: then see if it is a deformed fracture that does not break into two, and it is curved towards the inside: then the knowledge of its fracture is easy.
Therefore, if the fracture is on the right side of the jaw, then insert your left finger in the deformed right jaw: similarly, if the fracture is on the left side of the jaw, then insert your right hand and push the exterior part of the deformed fragment with ease from the inside with your other hand, and make it equal to the same.
If the jaw fracture has already been separated into two parts, then it is necessary to give an extension from two parts according to the straight line until it is possible to equalize it.
If, however, already there has been irritation or disruption in the teeth, then tighten those from which you hope that the gold wire, silver wire, or silk will remain. Then place a fracture above the broken mandible. Then place a double band over it, and place a large star-shaped needle above it, or a frustum of the sole's skin of equal length to the mandible.
Then tie it down from above, according to what you have prepared for the bandage of it, and make sure that its application conforms to it until it is not destroyed: and advise the patient to be quiet and calm: and put a soft food on its gums.
If, however, you think that something has already changed in the figure of some kind: then at once, and dissolve it on the third day. Then correct what has changed from it, and apply the emplasm on the dough with almond oil, or with flour of wheat, after you have removed the swelling: and put a soft wool on top of the emplasm.
While this emplasm is attached over it, and the bone position does not change, let it go, so that you do not dissolve it, until it heals: or tighten the fracture. For this fracture is often tightened in three weeks.
If, however, in the meantime an apoplexy hot occurs: then administer that which I said often for its sedation, until that apoplexy is removed.
And if there is a fracture with the tendon, consider it. If it has already been separated from the bone, or from the tendon: then use me to remove this sagged part with that which is convenient for you in removal of these from the instruments.
If I am correct, and the wound opening is tight, then enlarge it with a vein puncture according to the quantity required by your need.
Then when you will withdraw that abscess, and nothing remains of it in the wound, then make an opening for the wound if it is wide: and if not, put upon it from above from the plasters which are suitable for this, and make them fatten, until it heals.

¶ Regarding the restoration of the fracture of the Spatula:
¶ Rarely does the Spatula break, and in its side: and they do not break from it except its edges.
¶ When it is broken from that place, and it is broken in the middle of it, and it is not known unless by touch: then according to the figure of the fracture, prepare theSpatula. ¶ And reduce it over the natural figure of the whole skill that is possible for you.

¶ If there is any connection between the Spatula and the star in some places: then make equal to that connection with a soft stitch until the star falls according to the equality.
¶ Then cut under with a long amber thread, according to the fitting and strength of the star, until it is not removed from its place.
¶ And consider the bandage every day. ¶ Whenever it is softened the bandage, tie it up again, and make equal to the star if it is removed from its place: and let it be weak on the sound side.
¶ And the Spatula is indeed restored in nineteen days, or in nineteen and five days.
When this number is completed, then loosen the ligament: and you will be in security. For it is from bones whose union is not feared, nor their loosening.
If however, it proceeds from a bone fragment, and punctures the skin: then cut above it, and take it away. And do what I have said in the fracture of the goose's knee from heating with an aposteme, if something comes out of it.
¶ Regarding the restoration of the breast bone fracture.
The breast sometimes breaks in the middle of itself, and this seldom happens. However, the extremities of it are most broken and cut off from its accidents.
When the middle of it breaks, which leans to the lower parts, and there is pain in the chest, and difficulty in breathing, and coughing, and perhaps blood is vomited, and the connection of the broken bone is declared in the bone, and not hidden from the senses, the restoration of it is, that it be made to rise again on its own back: and place between the spatulas of it a piece of pulverized gypsum.
Then press the shoulders of it, and gather up the ribs of it from two sides or parts with your hands: and make them subtle according to their disproportion, according to all manner of how that can be done, until the figure of the bones returns, according to what is necessary.
Then place over it the plaster and bandage, and place under it a star from the thin sheet of salt, or chalicle, and from such like in softness, after you have applied it to the cloth. Then make the binding of it over the broken bone subtle, so that it may not be removed: and knot with the bandage according to the roundness to the back several times, and make it strict according to its decrepitude.
Then consider the ointment at all times, and as much as it is softened, tighten it: and if the need arises to solve it at the spot of the itch, or the crust that happens in the place, or the pain, or the ulcer: then immediately, and dissolve it, and remove the plaster, and rectify what happens with the manner of its treatment.
Then reduce the ointment, if you wish to see that face: and tie it to the tightened one, until it heals.
¶ Regarding the restoration of noses, when they are broken.
You know that the nose is broken only in the thick places which follow the back, and from the extremities of these on the front side. And it does not happen to them by itself, because they are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of it is not hidden from the senses during the examination with fingers.
And the restoration of it is made in such a way that the broken part is adjusted with fingers according to its possible form, until it is equal in figure according to what is necessary. Then the ointment, and tie the broken nose with a star, if that is needed for it.
And if the nose is broken downwards: then a greater pain and pustule occur, and the pustule is like that which happens to him who has pleurisy: therefore, because the nose punctures the pleurisy, and also difficulty of thirst, coughing, and bloody sputum, occurs to him: and this is difficult to cure.
And the ancients were already ingenious in it with many arts: and among them there are some who say that you should put weak foods which generate inflammation and heat, so that the stomach becomes inflamed, and extended, and the fracture is pushed to the outside.
We ourselves abhor this, so that no cause of injury be made for making a hot sty, unless it happens. If it has already happened, then add to it.
Certain of them said that a wet sponge should be placed over the spot, then pulled out with strength, and it is more like reasoning. Nevertheless, they fear that a wet sponge will attract excessive moisture to the spot due to the weakness of its position.
Certain of them said that it is necessary to cover the spot with wool that has already been dipped in hot oil: and they place flax between the ribs, until it is full: so that it becomes an equal ligature when it is wrapped around according to its roundness.
Then, a weak cure for pleurisy from food and medicine is applied.
If the weak body is not enduring, and the jaw is pierced by a sharp point, and we fear for the weak one, then it is necessary to cut over the spot, and detach the broken rib. Then we will place over it an instrument that keeps the suture, of which the remembrance precedes: and we will insert the jaw with ease, and insert it.
Then we will sew up two lip wounds, if it is a large one with suture: and we will treat it with plasters until it heals.
If a hot sty happens to occur in the meantime, then immediately, and pour flax into hot rose oil, and place over it: and treat the weak one with that which is suitable for the sty.
Meanwhile, also, it will heal over the side where it lies, until it heals.
¶ About the restoration of a spinal or neck fracture.
Indeed, sometimes a fracture occurs in the neck of the bone, and this happens rarely. Similarly, it also happens to the vertebrae of the back. When this happens to someone, and you want to know if it will heal or not, then trust in it. For if you see his hands already softened, dull, and lifeless, and cannot move them, nor stretch them, nor contract them: and when you contract them with your fingers, and prick them with a sharp instrument, they do not feel it, and do not feel any pain in them: then you know that it will not heal, and there is death in many positions for it.
If it moves them, and they feel the contraction with their fingers and the prick with a sharp instrument: then you know that the nape of the bones is healthy, and that the weak one will heal with care.
If you find the vertebrae of the back similar to this one, and you want to know if it will heal too, or not: then consider the feet of the person: then if you see that they are softened, and that happens to them what happens to the hands: then when it lies on its back, exhaustion and emaciation come without will: and when it is turned over on its belly, exhaustion comes without will: and when it is turned over on its back, and wants to join, and cannot: then you know that it is mortal. Therefore, do not worry about its care.
If it does not happen to him anything from that, it is better for him.
The treatment of his that happens from that is to place hot sedative bandages on the wrist: so that you put oil that has been rubbed with heat over the softened vertebrae: or when you cut it, cut it with a sharp instrument.
Place something over the aforementioned spot until the hot pain subsides: then place something over one of the comforting plasters, which dry and stretch it over the spot with a ligature: and instruct the patient to use quietness and tranquility. And let them not sleep on the part where they found pain, until it heals.
If something happens at the site of bone friction, or if something has already been separated from it, then it is necessary to remove that from the skin. Then gather two lips of the wound, if it is large, with the suture. Then care for it with some ointment, until it heals.

Regarding the restoration of the fracture of the Bragg's.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called the two foci.

¶ About the treatment of the nodification in the fracture of a bone.
This happens often in the way of this nodation in the path of healing of fractures. Especially, which is near the joints: and becomes deformed in its figure from this. And perhaps it may prevent the member from its natural operation.
Consider then, if the nodation is recent: administer to it medicines which have an styptic property, such as aloe, and olibanum, and myrrh, and sarcocolla, and acacia, and such like.
Thus, take some of these, or all of them: and confuse them with vinegar, or with albumen, or with acid: and place them over the nodation in the wound, and bind them firmly over the same stricture good.

Or bind it over the same lead plate. For lead has the property to remove everything which is feared from the members.
But if the nodation is already turned to the stone, and is strict, and a sudden necessity is required to remove it: then find over it from its upper part, and cut off the superfluous part: or grate it with some sharp instruments, until it be removed: and take care of it until it be healed.
¶ When it is healed, and the member remains sound after that, it does not happen according to its nature.
When the fracture of the bones is healed, and the member remains sound after that weak, it does not happen except for many causes. The treatment for this condition is to feed it with weak and tender food, until blood is multiplied in it. And apply a bath to it. And introduce joy and merry company to him.
Then place a plaster over the member: so that it may attract the plaster to the food in large amounts. And apply ointment to it with hot water, until the food flows out and returns to its natural shape.
¶ About the curing of broken bones when they twist.
When the member has already been restored after the healing of the bone and the fracture or nodation, and it becomes deformed due to the broken bone: and it is deformed in its shape, but the member is not prevented from performing its natural operation: then it does not need to accept the speech of him who thinks that the bone should be broken again.
And there have been many stupid doctors and restorers who have done this on our land.
And this operation is indeed shameful, leading to death or great fear, so that it may be deadly.
But if the claudication and nodation are recent: then it should be applied with hot water, in which decoctions of softening herbs have been added, such as the leaves of the high-growing plant, and its root, and melilot, and similar ones. And make for it a plaster with softening plaster, such as dyachilon made appropriately.
Or consider the mucilage of the high-growing plant, and mix it with chicken fat, or with olive oil: and apply it with that.
Or use ripe figs, and bind them with fine linen, and similar ones that are named from decreasing medicines to strengthen the consolidation.
When the knot is untied with constant, old, and gentle friction made with wet hands. And the motion of the limbs is given to every part in all hours.
If however, the knot has already become twisted, and has been made strong, and has struck against the stone, and has called for attention to its care with iron: then it should be cut off as above, and the continuity of bones should be released, and the excess from the knot or bone with the sharp knives should be cut off: and ease should be given in that place with anxiety and diligence.
Regarding speech in Dislocation.
Dislocation is the exit of some joint from its place. Because it is forbidden from its own motion, and its figure becomes deformed. And it causes severe pain and grievous wounds to come upon the weakest.
When then a dislocation occurs in someone, it is necessary to hurry to the restoration of that one: and there should be no delay at all.
For if it delays, the place will become postponed, and perhaps with it the restoration of the dislocation.
Therefore, it does not need to move, nor extend in the hour of its postponement. For it often causes a spasm and painful wounds.
But when that happens, it is necessary to hurry to the bleeding of the weak one. Then it is released, until the postponement has rested.
Then the limb is rubbed with warm water and oil. Then it is returned with ease.
And all the limbs are cared for with that one whose memory fails in its place.
I have already arranged the chapter of dislocation, even according to what precedes in the fracture, from the upper part of the body down to its lower part.
Regarding the care of the dislocation of the lower jaw.
The lower jaw is rarely dislocated except in two cases.
The dislocation of the lower jaw occurs according to one of two methods. Or they are removed from their places by a slight removal and are softened somewhat. Or they are dislocated in a complete and integral manner, until they are softened to the chest, until the weak jaw is curved, and it cannot hold it, nor close its mouth, and the tongue of its speaker is uncovered.
However, when the dislocation of it is small, it returns to many positions by itself with little quantity.
If however the dislocation is complete and integral, then it should be that the reduction of it is made quickly and not delayed: and it is necessary that it holds firmly the weak head: and the physician inserts his thumb of one hand into the root of the dislocated jaw between the teeth of it, if the dislocation is from one side. Or he inserts both of his hands at once, if the dislocation is from two sides. And the rest of his fingers are outside, by which he can equal it.
And he commands the weak one to clean his jaws and to free them for eating to all parts. And the physician can hold the jaw and push it, until it returns to its place.
If however the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are at once: then he should make a ointment with hot water and oil, until a easy reduction of it is made to the lower jaw: and he does not delay the reduction of it altogether, as we said.
When they finally fall asleep and close their wounds, and are not softened: then place some flax cloths over them, with a piece of wax that has been made from wax: and rose oil.
Then a bandage is applied with ease, using soft ligatures.
And the sleep of the weak should be on their back: and the head of it should stand between two flax cloths, so that it does not move to the right and left: nor is it compelled, or forced to chew anything: indeed, a food of its own desire is placed under its jaw, until when the pains subside, and the mandible is sutured: then it eats what it wants, and it is administered with ease.
It does not labor over the operation of its own mouth at eating and drinking, and the nodding, until the mandible is sutured, and it is healed.
If it is difficult to reduce the two mandibles when they are dislocated at some time and do not return to their places: then many fevers occur from there, and constant diet: and perhaps the weak belly is loosened, and perhaps the bitter purification of choler is vomited.
When you see that: then you know that it is a loss, and many die that are affected by this in ten days.
¶ About the reduction of the shoulder joint dislocation, and the extremity of the humerus.
The shoulder joint, however, is not dislocated from the inner side, because of its continuity with the chest. And it is sometimes dislocated to the outside: and this is declared by touch.
And the restoration of it is, that it lies weak on its back, and extends its arms. Then place it in a strong position with your palm, because it will return.
Then place the plaster over the same, and the bandages, and tie it tightly.
The extremities of these, which follow the thigh, and continue with it, are not easily dislocated, except rarely. If it is dislocated on any day: then it must be reduced and made equal as we have said, and prepared for you.
Then place the plaster over the same, the bandages, and the tied one. And instruct the surgeon to remain calm and quiet until it heals.
And when this same treatment is applied to the extremity of the shoulder, when it separates even from its own place.
¶ About the reduction of the shoulder dislocation.
You know that the shoulder is not dislocated unless in three ways.
One of which is, that it be dislocated to the side of the titillation towards the lower part. The second is, that it be dislocated to the side of the chest. And perhaps it is dislocated to the upper part of the shoulder: and this happens rarely.
And it is not dislocated to the back, because of the spatula. And it is not dislocated to the front, because of the nerves.
And it is most often dislocated and goes out to the lower part, turning away from the titillation: especially in those, where the flesh is few. For it goes out quickly in them, and comes quickly.
But in those where the flesh is many, it is opposite to that, for it comes out with difficulty, and goes in with difficulty.
And perhaps it happens in some men by blow or accident, and the shoulder is struck with hot iron, and it is thought that it is dislocated because of that.
It is therefore necessary to try this, until you are certain of it, and then proceed to treat it.
And the dislocation is known when the shoulder is turned towards the chest: and you distinguish between a dislocated shoulder and a sound one. For you find a clear difference between them, and you find a union in the head of the humerus, and under the titillation at the touch of the head of the humerus as if it were one bone: and an infirm person cannot make the hand move to their own ear, nor move it with all movements.
And similarly, if the shoulder is dislocated towards the chest or upwards: you will find a clear touch, and it does not hide.
And this dislocation is easy to reduce when it is recent or the child is weak.
The reduction of this is that he should raise his own hand to the upper part. Then place your hands under the titillation of this, and raise the joint with strength to the upper part of its own place: and he should raise his hand and extend it upwards. Then he will lower it down, for he returns quickly.
But if he does not return by what I have said, and the days that pass before it happens are many: then it is necessary to bathe the weak person in hot water, and apply an ointment that softens and soothes, as if the roots of the tall grasses, and the grapevine, and the mallow were rubbed in and put in the water, and applied.
Then the weak person will rise on his back: and a sphere and wool that is equal between softness and rigidity is placed under the titillation of this.
Then the physician will place his heel on the sphere, and raise the head of the humerus with strength, and draw the weak hand downwards.
If you wish, place him according to this manner: and let the present man be longer for the weak one, and stand on the side of the side, and insert his arm under the weakened shoulder: and lift the weakened shoulder upwards until it is suspended in the air. Another attendant should draw the weak hand towards the lower ventricle of him. If the weak one is lying on his back: then it is necessary that something else be hung from him, so that it weighs him down, because the dislocation returns immediately.
It is also reduced according to another manner: and it is that the log, whose head is round figure like the handle of a mortar, should be laid on the ground: that is not heavy, nor light. Then the cloths are laid on the weak one after the logs have been placed on his head: and the weak one should stand according to the length of the log.
Then the hand of him is extended towards the lower part of the other side, and his body is extended from the other side with strength: because the joint returns to its place with weakness.
If it is difficult to reduce him by all that I have said: then use this care: and it is that you take a log whose length is the quantity of two arms, and whose width is the quantity of four fingers, and whose thickness is the quantity of two fingers: whose head is round, so that the entrance of his shoulder goes into it deep, according to this form.
Then the soft cloths are tied over the round head: so that they do not hurt the weak one.
Then the weak one is laid on the weakened shoulder, so that the hand or arm extends over the log up to the lower parts: and the log is placed over the helper, the arm, and the end of the hand.
Then the arm is laid over the step of the ladder across its width, and the hand is extended to the lower part, and the rest of the body is let down from the other side. The joint immediately enters.
When the opening is completed as much as possible: then it is necessary to place under a flat sphere of equal quantity from wool. Then place the prepared plaster made from flour with olibanum and almond glue over the entire arm as it rotates.
Then tie the appropriate bandage: and let the rope rotate over the plaster: and connect the hand of this to the neck of its own, and let it hang, and do not allow the hand of this to move for seven days.
And it is necessary to place soft food, until the limb is strengthened, because it is more beneficial for its healing.
Then a larger one is placed after seven days, or five, and it is moved with motion. If it holds firm and is not softened, then it is already cured.
If the joint is dislocated many times, due to the moisture that happens to it, or for some other cause: then it is necessary to apply this cauterant with three holes, according to what was said in the chapter of cauterization.
If this is all done and the ligament is loosened after seven days, and the joint is not strengthened, and the plaster and bandage are applied again to it several times, and it does not hold, and it falls, and cannot be lifted up to the shoulder: then you know that the bones of its arm which are towards the head of the humerus are already cut, or stretched, or softened: and you know that the joint is never set in its place.
Dislocations, however, are caused by the force of pulling and extending the chest and breast, and they are reduced with impulse when it returns, and the remaining tightness and care are given until it is cured.
If, however, it occurs after cure and there is stiffness in the member and slowness in its movement, then a weak bath should be used many times until the stiffness is relieved and it returns to its original state.
¶ Treatment of the Dislocation of the Knee.
You know that the dislocation of the knee joint is difficult, and it also returns with difficulty, especially to all parts, and particularly to the front and back.
And its dislocation is not hidden from you: for it is falling under the use, and under the touch, according to whichever figure it is dislocated. And when you reduce the dislocated knee with the sound one, it is clearly shown to you. The joint is concealed, and it cannot double the arm, nor touch with its own elbow.
And it is necessary to hurry and reduce the dislocation immediately before it becomes a hot sprain.
For if a hot sprain occurs to it, the reduction becomes difficult: and perhaps it is not cured at all, especially when it is to the back. For it is worse in every kind of dislocation and more violent pain: and often it dies with it.
And the restoration of it is when it can be reduced by those that are possible to be reduced, and the hand of the patient is extended with two hands: and the arm of the patient is expanded, and the hand of the physician is above the knee and under it, and he presses the dislocation with his palms of his hands simultaneously, or with the root of his thumb, until it returns to its place.
If indeed it is a dislocation to the front, then it returns thusly, so that the hand doubles with its own hand, until it strikes with the root of its palm, which is dislocated, with its own little finger.
If the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer a strong extension, with firm hold: and it is, as if two hands were extending the arm, and both hold it, so that it does not move during extension.
Then the arm is turned over to all parts, after it has been reduced above the palm of the hand, whether it be a long doubled bandage or a wide ligament.
And when it touches the physician's hand with the joint, anoint it with oil: so that it be one of those which aid in the lubricity of the joint with ease. Then he will drive the joint with a strong push, until it returns.
And after the reduction of it, it is necessary that it be laid on the same plaster, wherein there is stipticity, and exudation with the bone: and it should be tied in a proper manner. And hang the arm from the weak neck: and let it remain for some days.
Then it is unfastened. If then the joint is firm in its place, then unfasten the ligament from it, and let it go.
And if you see the joint not to be well tied, then repeat the plaster, and the ligament, and let it go for some days, until it is tied. Then let it go.
If however, oldness comes to it after the reduction of the joint, and it is in motion: then administer hydration in the bath, and subtle friction, and compression, until it becomes tight, or lay it on the same joint, that is, the fat nates of a pig. Then, let it lie and release her on that day and night. Then, take it away and insert him into the bath. When he is washing, then scrape the joined part with gentle rubbing. Then, dip it over her again once, and twice, and a third time with the introduction of the bath, until it becomes soft.
And if you wish, place over her feces a damp, heated wax, and twist it over her.
Do this several times. She becomes softened, and returns to its original position.
¶ About the treatment of dislocations in Almahassem.
The hand of Mahassem is often dislocated, that is, the joint between the arm and the hand.
And the reduction of this dislocation is easy. The contrary of the others is jointed. Nevertheless, it is necessary that there be ease in the reduction of this dislocation at the time of its dislocation, before the place be seized; or it may happen in the seizure of the place a hot aposteme.
And the reduction of this dislocation is that you place Mahassem's weak hand over the table, and extend her other hand; and the physician places his palm over the prominence of the joint, and presses it until it returns.
Nevertheless, it is necessary that it be considered if the palm has already receded to the vent of the hand: then he places his weak back over the table at the extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is proceeding to the back of the hand, then the position of his hand intrinsically over the table is that the inner part of his hand is placed over the prominence of the joint, so that the hand of the physician falls on its own prominence of the joint.
If he returns immediately, it is good: and if not, then grasp it with ointment while it is seated, and let him go, without troubling about this.
For if he does not endure, nor can you supervise his reduction after he has left him for several days, nevertheless the union remains according to the texture of his own nature: and it will not hurt the weak at all, unless his hand is softened, and he cannot hold anything: then you know that the nerves are either ruptured or merely scratched.
It is not genius in this, unless it is held with ointment near the risk of its case. And perhaps it does not harm anything.
When Almahassem does not return, then place the ointment we have mentioned over him. Then it is grasped and let go for five days, and then it is loosened and let go, and the hand is exercised.
If however, the movement of his body is arrested, and something heavy happens to it within it: then ease it with warm water and friction several times, until it is eased.
¶ About the treatment of the dislocation of the digits.
When a digit is dislocated from any of them, or from the back of the palm, or from its ventrum: then extend the finger, and press the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns.
Then support the head of the finger and suspend the reversed part to which it is dislocated for two days, and let it go.
Then suspend it and extend it until it is straight in that day.
When it is night, tie it according to the description: and do not cease to untie it in the day: and exercise it with motion: and tie it at night.
This should be done after some days, until it is tightened.
Similarly, if it is dislocated to the belly of the hand, and you have done the first part of your operation, tighten it to its own part, and then do the operation again until it heals.
Similarly, when it is dislocated to other parts, do the operation as before.
¶ On the treatment of a dislocation of the Dorsospine.
When it happens to anyone that the Dorsospine is dislocated, or the complete dislocation of the neck, or the Spondiles are pulled out from their places: then there is no cure in this case, because death comes to the weak.
And the sign of this is that the weak one goes out without will, and cannot hold it, and often some members of him are softened, or two thighs of him, or two arms of his, or one of them: or the Spondile is pulled out from its place.
For it is often removed because it is often made to be removed to four parts.
Therefore, that which is removed to the back, is called Gibbosity.
The cure of it is, that you consider this now, if Gibbosity happens from childhood: then there is no cure in this case, neither is there any healing at all.
But in the other that happens by chance, or by hitting, or similar to these, the ancients have already spoken about the modes of curing with a long speech. Whose most does not return when beneficial.
I have already briefly spoken of it, which does not excuse much from much proximity to my house: and from my explanation, in which I explained that, and shaped the instrument of it in a different way from what they showed it, and explained it.
I declare that there is no intelligence or cure from the gangrene that occurs from the anterior part.
And similarly, in those that occur to both sides as well.
And it is not cured from them, unless those that happen in the back properly, as I say: and it is, as it extends over its face above the heel, nearly up to the wall: and it is expanded under it with a moist string, so as not to hurt the chest of its.
Then a straight log is placed on the ground, fixed in the heel at the end of the foot, nearly at the head of its: and another log is placed at the other end of its foot: and it should hold the log, and it should not be firmly fixed in the heel. And it should hold the other.
Then cover over the weak head, and under the ligament of its gently, tightly: and extend the end of the ligament to the log that is at its head: and tie it up in it.
Then stretch another string above the ankles of its, and above its knees, and at its heels. Then gather all the strings together, and tie them up in the other log that is at its feet.
Then the holder will extend all the strings to the part of the log ligament, and the two logs should not be removed from their places where they are fixed: but they should not be firmly fixed, as I said.
And the physician will put his hands on the spine with strength, until he returns: or he will put it over a table. Then he will tie his feet to the table until he returns.
If however he does not return by this treatment, then take the table whose length is about three arms. The surgical incision should be made on the wall near the weakest point, where the table's edge enters. Then place the middle of the table on the spine. The physician should then place his feet on the opposite end of the table. The physician should tie it tightly until the spine is compressed and return to his place.
Then, if you wish, operate with the hand that is wrapped around your arm: and it is as if you are placing two sticks on the ground near the weakest part of the foot, where the length of one brachium is equal to the length of the other.
And the length of the stick between all the fingers of your hand should be equal to one palm: and already there is a hole in every place where the tornum runs: and it should be firmly planted on the ground a lot, so that it does not move at all.
And then he inserts the round stick: and the tornum is where the ligament rotates in two holes of two sticks.
And at the end of it there is a hole where the stick is firmly planted, whose length is equal to the hand that rotates it, and in two other sticks it is also like this.
Then tie the ligaments that you have tied in the weak chest of the tornum that is at the head of it: and tie the ligaments that you have tied in the legs of it in the tornum that is at the foot of it.
Then stand at all the minor tornums that rotate the hand of your leg with the ligament, and the physician should compress the spine according to what we have said.
This is the form of the tornum that follows.
Then after the spine is compressed and returns to its place, then it is necessary to put the plaster of ichor with albumin on the wound. Then he applied a dressing. Then place above the plaster a star-shaped piece from the tablet, whose width is three digits, or about them. And the length of it should be such that it occupies the place of Gibbositatis, and above a part of Sanae's spine.
Then it is placed with the bandage that we mentioned. And a weak nourishment of fine substance is used until it heals.
If there remains some prominence in the place at the end of healing: then it is necessary that the medicines which soften and ease be given, with the administration of the tablet that we mentioned at a long time. And already the lead is being administered in that layer.
And sometimes there is a prominence in the end of the spine of the back: and it is supposed to be a dislocation, and that is the bone that has already projecting. Therefore it does not belong to be presumed to it with this medicine, perhaps it will cause death to happen.
¶ About the treatment of Anchae's dislocation.
You know that the joints of Anchae and the joints of the elbow do not happen unless it is a dislocation only: nor does it happen to them which happens to other joints by removal small and conduction.
And indeed the joint of Anchae is dislocated according to four parts. For what reason it is that it is dislocated to the front and back.
And indeed it is mostly dislocated to the inside: and very little to the front and back.
And the treatment of the dislocation of it to the inside is: because when you join the weak thigh with a sound one with a weak one, it is longer: and the thigh of his is more prominent than the sound one, and the weak one cannot extend its foot at the inguinal area: and it is the place which follows the inguinal area struck by fire, because the head of the hip has already been soaked there.
The mark of this is that a dislocation occurs on its outside, which are its opposite circumstances.
The mark of this is that a dislocation occurs in the front, because it extends the sound thigh according to the complement: indeed it does not double itself without pain which is in the knee: and if it strives to walk, it cannot go over that in front: and it retains its urine, and it is pinched at its buttock, and there is a cushioning of its thigh at its heel during walking.
The mark of this is that a dislocation occurs in the back, as it does not extend the thigh, nor can it double itself before doubling the knee: and its thigh is also shorter on the other side: and the buttock is softened, and the head of the hip is at that place.
Reduction, however, of this dislocation is that you should consider at that time, if the dislocation is habitual, which is already old with having it. Then, if you perform a reduction of it, and it remains according to its disposition: then there is no cure at all in it. Therefore, it does not follow that there should be a presumption of great pain.
In that one whose dislocation is recent, and it is of one of the four kinds of dislocations: then promptly to this, if you want to join it, extend it to the interior and to the exterior, and move it right and left: perhaps it will return, and there will be no need for another care.
If, however, it does not return in this way, then it is necessary to prepare a strong attendant, and extend the thigh of its lower part: or with its hands, or with the bandage that is laid on its thigh above the knee: and another attendant extends its hand over the fingers of its, so that it pushes its own hand under the fingers of its. Then he ties it with a gentle bandage over the root of the hip bone, and holds onto the other end of the ligament.
And its extension should be from the front of the thigh to the shoulder or from the back to the back.
And their extensions should be of equal length until the weak one hangs down with his body from the earth and remains hanging.
For this is the common form of the four kinds of extensions.
If then the displacement returns with that which I said, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary for every kind of this that I will say about its treatment.
In the reduction itself of that kind, when the displacement is to the inside, it is necessary for the weak one to be hung over the sound side of its own. Then he will put the bandage over the root of the hip bone, in the part that is between the head of the hip bone and the place under the thigh. Then he will extend the bandage upwards from the front of the thigh to a higher part of the body, to the shoulder.
Then he will take another strong arm for him, and will reduce the large part of the weak thigh, and will extend it to a strong extension. And now it returns to its own place.
For this is the easiest of the other kinds of treatment for this member.
If however, he is excused from you and does not obey you to enter with this kind of treatment altogether: then it is necessary for two weak feet to be tied together with a strong and gentle bandage over two calcanei, and over two knees; and the length of each should be of the same size as the four digits of its own quantity: and the weak one should be extended more by the quantity of two digits.
Then the weak one is hung up on the head of the wooden beam, which is at home: and its length is equal to the quantity of two forearms.
Then have the strong man of the youth press the head of the thigh: and let it hang with the weak one at another place: and let the strong man of the youth push the thigh with strength, for the joint returns to its place with weakness.
In the reduction of its own joint, when it is dislocated to the outside, it is necessary that the weak one lies on the foot, according to what we have said about the genuine deformity. And the tendon is tied above the thigh of the weak one, properly, and above his breast.
Then two beams are placed, one at his foot, and another at the head of the same. Then a beam is placed in the middle of the thigh, fixed well: over which wet bandages are placed so that it does not hurt the weak one: and the beam should be between the thighs so that it does not attract to the lower parts during extension.
Then all the helpers extend to their own part, and the physician ensures the reduction with his hand.
If then he obeys to the reduction, it will be good.
And if not, place over the same tablet, and press it equally as we have said in the deformity, on the same. However, it is necessary that the weak one lies on his own sound side.
And when the dislocation is to the front, then it is necessary that the weak thigh is stretched out carelessly: and this is according to the same statement over the thigh: and the physician places his right hand palm on the weak groin. Then he bends it with his other hand, and places it extended to the lower part of the same leg.
If the dislocation is to the back: then it is not necessary for the weak one to extend to the lower parts, and to be raised above the ground: rather, it should be placed above a hard object, just as it is necessary when it is also dislocated wide on the outside, according to what we said about his pillow over two bellies: it is above his face, and the ligaments are extended: according to what we said earlier.
And it should be given compression with the table, even above the place to which it is going to be rejoined, when the reduction is complete according to what is necessary.
And the sign of the reduction will not be hidden from you: and it is that the weak ones extend two legs: and when you see them equal, and the weak one strings his own leg, and expands it without excuse: then you know that the member has already returned, according to what is necessary: then join the two thighs, and put on plaster, and tie with a tight rope, so that it does not move widely to any part: and the weak one should hold tight for three days, or four.
Then untie the ligament, and the plaster, and compare the leg with the other: then if you see them equal in state, then you know that the dislocation is already firm: therefore, the weak one should be able to walk.
And if you see something of softening: then return, and the plaster of that, and tie it according to your first tightness: and let it be loose for three days.
Then untie that and make it slow in walking over the other for a few days until it is well recovered.
¶ About the dislocation of the Knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways. The dislocation occurs on the outside, inside, and bottom, that is, to the back.
And it is not dislocated to the front.
The sign of this dislocation is that it is bent downwards, as if to apply its thigh to its own hip. However, if it does not adhere to the hip: then you know that the knee is dislocated.
The restoration of all modes of this dislocation is to make the weak one sit upright, so that it may extend its thigh as far as possible, and your back may be against its face if it can, and place its foot between your feet.
Then make it cling to the knee of your hand, and make the fingers of your hand straight above the knee. Then apply with your hand's palm the two sides of the knee with strength. And another extends its foot, until it returns to its place.
The sign of return is that it clings to the thigh with the hip in ease, not laboriously.
Then apply the plaster itself, and make it cling to the thigh with the hip. Then bind it together for three or four days. Then untie it.
And the weak one should not use walking for a few days until it is cured.
However, if it refuses your reduction, then administer a strong extension with the ligaments that precede the story of Anchaea, until it returns.
¶ Treatment of Dislocation of the Heel (Calcaneus)
The heel is sometimes reduced by a small removal, and sometimes it is dislocated according to the complement. And its dislocation is made, either to the inside or outside.
The sign of this dislocation is that you see the calcaneus swollen, proceeding to the part to which it is dislocated.
The treatment of this displacement is a easy reduction: and it is that it extends with ease with the hands, and becomes equal, until it returns.
In the treatment of this however when it is dislocated according to the complement, it is necessary that you make the weak one sit upright: and hold it with a strong assistant from the posterior part of the back of it, in the middle of itself.
Then hold it with your right hand leg of it from the upper part of it, and with your left hand from the lower part of the foot of it in the place of the calf.
Then take the leg with your right hand. Then with your left two fingers.
Then extend it with your left hand, and press the chest of the foot with your right hand without effort.
Do this twice as we have told you.
Then press the chest of the foot to the thigh in your third finger, and you will pull the calf.
If then it returns to the thigh in one finger, or in two according to this way, and you see the leg equal, it is good: and if not, then you repeat the operation on it, because it returns itself.
If however it is forbidden you the reduction of it with that which we have told you: then make the weak one lie down on its back over its own back over the earth, and fasten a cushion in a firm earth, so that it falls between its legs: and you have already covered over it with a cloth, so that it does not hurt the weak one.
Then the assistant takes the chair of it: then extend the assistant's other leg, either with his hands or with a bandage that lies over the collar of the foot. Then the surgeon extends all his hands against the extension of his opponent's: and a splint is to be stood still between two weak legs, holding it, so that it does not attract the body of his opponent towards the lower parts at the extension.
Then the surgeon should reduce the dislocation with his hands, and the other should hold a sound leg to the lower parts. For dislocation returns with violence.
When then the dislocation returns, and you are declared healthy of the recovery thereof: then place the plaster and ointment, and tightly tie it with the firm ligaments, and tie the foot with the ligament to the thigh.
And it is necessary that you should beware or fear lest the nerves which are above the heel posterior should be harmed, and the ligament not be placed over them, lest they be injured.
Then release it to them in two or three days. If the ligament is softened, tie it up again: then dissolve it on the third or fourth day: and forbid the weak one from walking for forty days. If however he dares to walk before that time, it is not secure that the ligament may not be dissolved over the dislocation, and corrupted, and not receive after that treatment.
If however a hot aposteme happens to him: then it is necessary that you should give him in the sedative of his, in which our narrative precedes in another place from the cure, and ointment, until it be removed.
¶ About the dislocation of the toes of the foot, and care.
It is necessary that he be equal to what is dislocated from them with the extension of a little without labor: and that indeed is not difficult, but rather easy.
If however the dislocation is in some of the nails of the sole of the foot: then it is necessary that he make his weak foot sit on a place equal to the earth, or on a table: and stand as walking.
Then you stand still and place your foot on that which is being discussed from among those joints. Then heat that with your foot while you wait, and you will see it already even, and there will be no sign of an eminence in its place.
Then place under your foot's heel a tablet which will take the whole foot, with two heads on it. Then tighten it accordingly, after three days. Then loosen it, and make it rest from walking for many days, until it is tightened and secured from reduction.
¶ About the kinds of dislocations with a bone or fracture.
When something happens from that, and you take care of its cure and restoration, death often follows: and therefore it is not proper to presume upon the cure of that kind, unless he who does so be very skilled in the art, having long practice, merciful, pious, slow, not audacious, nor presumptuous: and to use in medicines which are laid up hot only: and to let go the weak for a long time. Unless you see, or hope for health from the salvation of pain and in which something seems to hope: Therefore, let him try the reduction of that immediately at the beginning of the case, before the hot aposteme comes.
If then the member returns according to our will, then we administer the regimen which soothes the hot aposteme: and we care for the bone with it that is convenient to him from dry unguents.
But if the dislocation is with a fracture, and happen in bones that are separated from each other, then let him try the removal of those, and make in it what I said in simple diseases from that which precedes in those places.
According to your virtue, and to keep you safe from the beginning of life to the end of it, fear nothing. This is according to what I have said before.
For this is better for you before God, and more lasting for your glory, and more safe for your body among them at their time.
